                    }
                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Haplotype-resolved genome analyses of a heterozygous diploid potato"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2020 52:10"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-09-28"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Potato (Solanum tuberosum L.) is the most important tuber crop worldwide. Efforts are underway to transform the crop from a clonally propagated tetraploid into a seed-propagated, inbred-line-based hybrid, but this process requires a better understanding of potato genome. Here, we report the 1.67-Gb haplotype-resolved assembly of a diploid potato, RH89-039-16, using a combination of multiple sequencing strategies, including circular consensus sequencing. Comparison of the two haplotypes revealed ~2.1% intragenomic diversity, including 22,134 predicted deleterious mutations in 10,642 annotated genes. In 20,583 pairs of allelic genes, 16.6% and 30.8% exhibited differential expression and methylation between alleles, respectively. Deleterious mutations and differentially expressed alleles were dispersed throughout both haplotypes, complicating strategies to eradicate deleterious alleles or stack beneficial alleles via meiotic recombination. This study offers a holistic view of the genome organization of a clonally propagated diploid species and provides insights into technological evolution in resolving complex genomes. Haplotype-resolved assembly of a heterozygous diploid potato, RH89-039-16, and identification of deleterious mutations provide insights into the genome organization and breeding of a clonally propagated diploid species."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Potato (<i>Solanum tuberosum</i> L.) is the most important tuber crop worldwide. Efforts are underway to transform the crop from a clonally propagated tetraploid into a seed-propagated, inbred-line-based hybrid, but this process requires a better understanding of potato genome. Here, we report the 1.67-Gb haplotype-resolved assembly of a diploid potato, RH89-039-16, using a combination of multiple sequencing strategies, including circular consensus sequencing. Comparison of the two haplotypes revealed ~2.1% intragenomic diversity, including 22,134 predicted deleterious mutations in 10,642 annotated genes. In 20,583 pairs of allelic genes, 16.6% and 30.8% exhibited differential expression and methylation between alleles, respectively. Deleterious mutations and differentially expressed alleles were dispersed throughout both haplotypes, complicating strategies to eradicate deleterious alleles or stack beneficial alleles via meiotic recombination. This study offers a holistic view of the genome organization of a clonally propagated diploid species and provides insights into technological evolution in resolving complex genomes.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Tetrasomic inheritance and clonal propagation via tubers are two structural challenges in <i>S. tuberosum</i> L. breeding and propagation. Genetic analyses in tetraploids are very complicated and thus genetic gains in potato breeding are limited. The widespread use of century-old varieties, such as Russet Burbank (a somatic mutant bred from a cultivar released in the 1870s in the United States) and Bintje (bred in 1904 in the Netherlands)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Ramulu, K. S., Dijkhuis, P. &amp; Roest, S. Phenotypic variation and ploidy level of plants regenerated from protoplasts of tetraploid potato (Solanum tuberosum L. cv. ‚ÄòBintje‚Äô). Theor. Appl. Genet. 65, 329‚Äì338 (1983)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4680e640">1</a></sup>, indicates that there has been little progress in developing key traits, such as yield, quality and disease resistance in modern tetraploids.</p><p>To accelerate genetic improvement in potato, several projects have been initiated to redomesticate potato from a tuber-propagated, tetraploid crop into a seed-propagated, inbred-line-based diploid crop<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lindhout, P. et al. Towards F1 hybrid seed potato breeding. Potato Res. 54, 301‚Äì312 (2011)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4680e647">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jansky, S. H. et al. Reinventing potato as a diploid inbred line-based crop. Crop Sci. 56, 1412‚Äì1422 (2016)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4680e647_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Y., Li, G., Li, C., Qu, D. &amp; Huang, S. Prospects of diploid hybrid breeding in potato. Chin. Potato J. 27, 96‚Äì99 (2013)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4680e647_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stokstad, E. Breeders seek a breakthrough to help farmers facing an uncertain future. Science 363, 574‚Äì577 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4680e650">5</a></sup>. To facilitate inbred line development, an improved understanding of the genome landscape of potato clones is required. While genome heterozygosity in diploid potato has been surveyed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189‚Äì195 (2011)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4680e654">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Aversano, R. et al. The Solanum commersonii genome sequence provides insights into adaptation to stress conditions and genome evolution of wild potato relatives. Plant Cell 27, 954‚Äì968 (2015)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4680e654_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hardigan, M. A. et al. Genome diversity of tuber-bearing Solanum uncovers complex evolutionary history and targets of domestication in the cultivated potato. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 114, 9999‚Äì10008 (2017)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4680e654_2">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Leisner, C. P. et al. Genome sequence of M6, a diploid inbred clone of the high-glycoalkaloid-producing tuber-bearing potato species Solanum chacoense, reveals residual heterozygosity. Plant J. 94, 562‚Äì570 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4680e657">9</a></sup>, these efforts were limited to bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) clones and short-read sequences and lacked a genome-wide assessment of haplotype diversity.</p><p>Despite recent advances in genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, W. B. &amp; Schneeberger, K. The impact of third-generation genomic technologies on plant genome assembly. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 36, 64‚Äì70 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4680e664">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiao, W. B. et al. Improving and correcting the contiguity of long-read genome assemblies of three plant species using optical mapping and chromosome conformation capture data. Genome Res. 27, 778‚Äì786 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4680e667">11</a></sup>, construction of a haplotype-resolved genome for highly heterozygous species remains a challenge<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kyriakidou, M., Tai, H. H., Anglin, N. L., Ellis, D. &amp; Stromvik, M. V. Current strategies of polyploid plant genome sequence assembly. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 1660 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4680e671">12</a></sup>. Current phasing strategies rely on the alignment of sequenced reads to a reference genome to infer regional haplotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Biernacka, J. M. et al. Assessment of genotype imputation methods. BMC Proc. 3, S5 (2009)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4680e675">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ullah, E. et al. Comparison and assessment of family- and population-based genotype imputation methods in large pedigrees. Genome Res. 29, 125‚Äì134 (2019)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4680e675_1">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050‚Äì1054 (2016)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4680e675_2">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mostovoy, Y. et al. A hybrid approach for de novo human genome sequence assembly and phasing. Nat. Methods 13, 587‚Äì590 (2016)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4680e675_3">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Weisenfeld, N. I., Kumar, V., Shah, P., Church, D. M. &amp; Jaffe, D. B. Direct determination of diploid genome sequences. Genome Res. 27, 757‚Äì767 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4680e678">17</a></sup>; such efforts are limited by the continuity of an available reference assembly. Koren et al. have developed an alternative approach, trio binning, that can recover both parental haplotypes from an F<sub>1</sub> individual by partitioning parental unique reads before assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Koren, S. et al. De novo assembly of haplotype-resolved genomes with trio binning. Nat. Biotechnol. 36, 1174‚Äì1182 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4680e684">18</a></sup>, in which case parental information is required. Recently, high-throughput/resolution chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) technology has helped to provide allele-resolved assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhang, J. et al. Allele-defined genome of the autopolyploid sugarcane Saccharum spontaneum L. Nat. Genet. 50, 1565‚Äì1573 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4680e689">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhang, X., Zhang, S., Zhao, Q., Ming, R. &amp; Tang, H. Assembly of allele-aware, chromosomal-scale autopolyploid genomes based on Hi-C data. Nat. Plants 5, 833‚Äì845 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4680e692">20</a></sup>.</p><p>The heterozygous diploid potato <i>S. tuberosum</i> group Tuberosum RH89-039-16 (2<i>n</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ2<i>x</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ24, hereafter referred as to RH; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">1</a>) has a pedigree from dihaploidized tetraploid commercial varieties, such as Katahdin, Chippewa and Primura. RH was partially assembled by the Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium (PGSC) in 2011 (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189‚Äì195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4680e711">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="de Boer, J. M. et al. Homologues of potato chromosome 5 show variable collinearity in the euchromatin, but dramatic absence of sequence similarity in the pericentromeric heterochromatin. BMC Genomics 16, 374 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4680e714">21</a></sup>). To resolve the RH genome at the haplotype level, we sequenced it using Illumina whole-genome sequencing (WGS), 10x Genomics (10xG) linked-read sequencing, Oxford Nanopore Technologies (ONT) and Hi-C technology (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">1</a>). However, our attempts to de novo assemble the two haplotypes of RH using ONT reads, and scaffolding using Hi-C reads, were unsuccessful (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">2</a>). Thus, we developed an integrated strategy to generate a haplotype-resolved assembly (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig1">1a,b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">3</a>). First, the diploid genome was assembled into scaffolds using Illumina reads (WGS and 10xG data; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">3</a>). Second, an RH selfing population was sequenced to provide genetic information to phase the assembled fragments. Through the genetic groupings, we assigned the scaffolds into 24 linkage groups, corresponding to the 12 chromosome pairs of RH (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">4</a>‚Äì<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">6</a>). Last, for each linkage group, the ONT and 10xG reads were retrieved and reassembled to generate an improved scaffold assembly. After polishing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Vaser, R., Soviƒá, I., Nagarajan, N. &amp; ≈†ikiƒá, M. Fast and accurate de novo genome assembly from long uncorrected reads. Genome Res. 27, 737‚Äì746 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4680e744">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Wang, J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4680e747">23</a></sup>, the hybrid assembly yielded the genome draft version 1.0 (RHgv1) with a total length of 1.69‚ÄâGb and a scaffold N50 length of 920‚Äâkb.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Hybrid de novo assembly and phasing of the diploid potato genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Hybrid de novo assembly and phasing of the diploid potato genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0699-x/MediaObjects/41588_2020_699_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0699-x/MediaObjects/41588_2020_699_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="777" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>,<b>b</b>, The genome draft RHgv1 was assembled from WGS Illumina reads and 10xG linked reads, and the derived scaffolds were assigned into 24 haplotype-specific groups through genetic mapping based on a sequenced F<sub>2</sub> population. The 24 groups represent chromosomes of the diploid potato (2<i>n</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ24). ONT reads were aligned to each linkage group and assembled to improve scaffold contiguity. <b>c</b>,<b>d</b>, A second genome sequence, RHgv2, was assembled from CCS reads. Similarly, unitigs were assigned into 24 groups through genetic mapping. <b>e</b>,<b>f</b>, The two assemblies were merged to generate a more comprehensive genome, RHgv3. Hi-C data were used to scaffold the sequences of each group into pseudochromosomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Recently, accurate circular consensus sequencing (CCS) has provided impressive results on assembly and variant detection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wenger, A. M. et al. Accurate circular consensus long-read sequencing improves variant detection and assembly of a human genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 37, 1155‚Äì1162 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4680e798">24</a></sup>, showing its potential in resolving complex genome regions. Here, we generated 29‚ÄâGb of CCS data and assembled them using CANU<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722‚Äì736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4680e802">25</a></sup>, resulting in 1.53‚ÄâGb unitigs (contigs, split at alternate paths in the assembly graph) with an N50 size of 2.19‚ÄâMb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">4</a>). Assisted by the RH selfing population, 1.31‚ÄâGb of unitigs were assigned into 24 groups, termed version 2.0 (RHgv2; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig1">1c,d</a>).</p><p>We assessed the accuracy of RHgv1 and RHgv2 using previously generated paired BAC-ends (BEs) and BAC clones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189‚Äì195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4680e815">6</a></sup>. In total, 95% and 99% of 54,902 BEs support the sequence correctness of RHgv1 and RHgv2, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">5</a>). In a total of 184 BACs previously assembled and ordered, 126 and 169 BACs mapped to a single fragment on RHgv1 and RHgv2, respectively, with 113 and 152 BACs showing perfect collinearity with the scaffolds or unitigs (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM3">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM4">2</a>). Notably, there were 10 BACs showing structural disagreements with both RHgv1 and RHgv2, while the latter two assemblies shared consistent structure, indicating potential errors in previous BAC assembly. The evaluation of base-level accuracy assessed by aligned BAC sequences was 99.127% and 99.936% for RHgv1 and RHgv2, respectively. Taken together, RHgv2 outperforms RHgv1 on both sequence continuity and accuracy.</p><p>To ensure the completeness of the final genome assembly, RHgv1 and RHgv2 were combined, generating a new 1.67‚ÄâGb assembly with an N50 length of 1.74‚ÄâMb (RHgv3; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">5</a>). After grouping by genetic map and anchoring by the Hi-C data, 1.62‚ÄâGb of sequence constituted 24 pseudochromosomes, exceeding current assemblies of potato genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189‚Äì195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4680e834">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Aversano, R. et al. The Solanum commersonii genome sequence provides insights into adaptation to stress conditions and genome evolution of wild potato relatives. Plant Cell 27, 954‚Äì968 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4680e837">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Leisner, C. P. et al. Genome sequence of M6, a diploid inbred clone of the high-glycoalkaloid-producing tuber-bearing potato species Solanum chacoense, reveals residual heterozygosity. Plant J. 94, 562‚Äì570 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4680e840">9</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig1">1e,f</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">7</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">6</a>‚Äì<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM5">3</a>). Hereafter, all analyses were performed on RHgv3.</p><p>The phasing quality of the haplotype-resolved assembly was assessed on pseudochromosomes of RHgv3. The BEs were realigned to chromosomes and 46,058 (95.1%) of 48,410 aligned BEs were in same phase. A total of 1,639 BACs with unordered contigs were used to assess haplotype partitioning. Among 1,624 BACs that mapped with at least 60% of BAC length, 1,573 BACs (96.8%) aligned to a single phase of the RH assembly. Previously, Boer et al. reported a phased assembly of RH chromosome 5 based on BAC-by-BAC sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="de Boer, J. M. et al. Homologues of potato chromosome 5 show variable collinearity in the euchromatin, but dramatic absence of sequence similarity in the pericentromeric heterochromatin. BMC Genomics 16, 374 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4680e863">21</a></sup>. The alignment of 26.7-Mb haplotype RH{0} BAC minimal tiling paths (MTPs) and 25.0-Mb haplotype RH{1} MTPs with the pseudochromosome chr5_1 and chr5_2 showed that 26.0-Mb (97%) RH{0} MTPs have the best hit on chromosome 5_1 and 23.9-Mb (96%) RH{1} MTPs have the best hit on chromosome 5_2. Collectively, these analyses demonstrated that the accuracy of haplotype determination is more than 95% at the chromosome level.</p><p>A total of 76,394 protein-coding genes were annotated in the RH genome, and evaluation with BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210‚Äì3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4680e870">26</a></sup> genes revealed that 97.0% (1,398) of 1,440 examined genes were complete, with 74.1% (1,306) duplicated. Comparative analyses among the gene models of RH, M6 (a diploid potato with an assembled genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Leisner, C. P. et al. Genome sequence of M6, a diploid inbred clone of the high-glycoalkaloid-producing tuber-bearing potato species Solanum chacoense, reveals residual heterozygosity. Plant J. 94, 562‚Äì570 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4680e874">9</a></sup>) and DM (the potato reference genome) identified 18,377, 3,842 and 10,742 lineage-specific genes in the three genomes, respectively, constituting 24.1%, 10.2% and 27.5% of their annotated genes, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">8</a>). For example, the dominant tuber shape gene <i>Ro</i>, which was reported absent in the DM clone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wu, S. et al. A common genetic mechanism underlies morphological diversity in fruits and other plant organs. Nat. Commun. 9, 4734 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4680e884">27</a></sup>, has two homozygous copies (<i>RHC10H1G1859.2</i> and <i>RHC10H2G2643.2</i>) on two RH haplotypes and has one copy (<i>g7634.t1</i>) on M6.</p><p>To provide an accurate evaluation of the divergence between the two RH haplotypes, we identified polymorphisms between the 12 homologous chromosome pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig2">2a</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">10</a> and Extended Data Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig4">1</a>‚Äì<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig13">10</a>). Based on the alignment of genes on the two haplotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4680e917">28</a></sup>, 198 syntenic blocks were detected, covering 1.3‚ÄâGb (80.2%) of anchored sequence (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">10</a>). Between syntenic blocks, 12,299,445 SNPs, 1,393,680 indels (~1‚Äì50‚Äâbp), 38,999 structural variants (SVs, &gt;50‚Äâbp) and 1,878 genes showing presence and absence variation (PAV) were identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4680e924">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Harris, R. S. Improved Pairwise Alignment of Genomic DNA. PhD thesis, The Pennsylvania State University (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4680e927">30</a></sup>, including 106 large SVs spanning more than 100‚Äâkb. Overall, the intragenomic diversity was estimated at ~2.1%, a level higher than the diversity among out-crossing maize lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289‚Äì1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4680e931">31</a></sup>. Based on synteny and annotation, 59,907 genes (78.4% of all annotated genes) were identified as having homologs on the two haplotypes, and 20,583 pairs (41,166 genes) of those were considered as reliable allelic genes. Among them, alleles of 17,092 gene pairs showed variants within the coding sequence (CDS), including amino acid alternation and premature termination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single-nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strain w1118; iso-2; iso-3. Fly 6, 80‚Äì92 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4680e935">32</a></sup>. Based on amino acid conservation modeling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Vaser, R., Adusumalli, S., Leng, S. N., Sikic, M. &amp; Ng, P. C. SIFT missense predictions for genomes. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1‚Äì9 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4680e939">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Kumar, P., Henikoff, S. &amp; Ng, P. C. Predicting the effects of coding non-synonymous variants on protein function using the SIFT algorithm. Nat. Protoc. 4, 1073‚Äì1081 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4680e942">34</a></sup>, 4,761 and 1,753 pairs of allelic genes were predicted to have potential deleterious substitutions in one and both alleles, respectively, indicating substantial accumulation of mutations in this clonally propagated crop<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ramu, P. et al. Cassava haplotype map highlights fixation of deleterious mutations during clonal propagation. Nat. Genet. 49, 959‚Äì963 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4680e947">35</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Haplotype divergence in a diploid potato genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Haplotype divergence in a diploid potato genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0699-x/MediaObjects/41588_2020_699_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0699-x/MediaObjects/41588_2020_699_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="666" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The central blue bars represent the two haplotypes of chromosome 1. The gray lines indicate paired allelic genes. Distribution of deleterious or dysfunctional mutations (brown), annotated genes (yellow), preferentially expressed alleles (green), methylation level of three contexts and differentially methylated regions are arranged symmetrically for each haplotype. Methylation level and the number of DMRs of methylated sites in CG (light blue), CHG (red) and CHH (orange) contexts are indicated by cumulative column chart. The number of DMRs on one haplotype only involves the DMRs with hypermethylation. All numbers were determined in 200-kb windows. <b>b</b>, Zoomed-in view of a syntenic block showing the mosaic pattern of preferentially expressed alleles (dark green) and alleles with lower expression (light green) on the two haplotypes.</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM6">Source data</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To understand the expression landscape of allelic genes, RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) data of ten tissues were analyzed using Kallisto pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Bray, N. L., Pimentel, H., Melsted, P. &amp; Pachter, L. Near-optimal probabilistic RNA-seq quantification. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 525‚Äì528 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4680e987">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ram√≠rez-Gonz√°lez, R. H. et al. The transcriptional landscape of polyploid wheat. Science 361, eaar6089 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4680e990">37</a></sup>. Overall, 48,361 genes (63.3% of total) expressed in at least one tissue and 3,417 gene pairs (16.6% of allelic genes) exhibited unequal expression between two alleles, termed differentially expressed loci (DEL). The DEL were distributed randomly throughout the genome with higher and lower expressed alleles occurring alternatively on the two haplotypes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">12</a>). From methylation sequencing of mature leaves, immature small tubers (transection diameter &lt;1‚Äâcm) and immature large tubers (transection diameter ~1‚Äì5‚Äâcm), on average, 24,929 differential methylated regions (DMRs) were identified between paired syntenic regions, resulting in allelic methylation differences in 6,345 gene pairs (30.8% of allelic genes; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">11</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Xi, Y. &amp; Li, W. BSMAP: whole-genome bisulfite sequence MAPping program. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 232 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1003">38</a></sup>. By comparing the DMRs with the DEL, we found the methylation difference explained only a fraction of the expression difference of alleles. For example, in immature small tuber tissue, only 292 DEL (27.5% of all DEL) showed both differential expression and methylation (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><p>Through the analysis of an RH selfing population, 25.7% of genomic regions (430.8‚ÄâMb) exhibited strong segregation distortion (SD; <i>œá</i><sup>2</sup> test, <i>P</i>‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ0.001; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">13</a>). Large-effect recessive deleterious mutations are the main cause of zygotic selection, which caused 71.4% of the SD regions. Using the selfed progeny of RH, we identified several loci affecting survival (white seedling 1 (<i>ws1</i>), abnormal rooting 1 (<i>ar1</i>), lethal allele 2 (<i>la2</i>)) or growth vigor (plant architecture 1 (<i>pa1</i>), plant architecture 2 (<i>pa2</i>) and weak vigor 1 (<i>wv1</i>)). Except for <i>wv1</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">14</a>), the other five loci have been previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhang, C. et al. The genetic basis of inbreeding depression in potato. Nat. Genet. 51, 374‚Äì378 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1050">39</a></sup>. Here, we relocated these loci on the phased RH genome, clarifying which haplotype contained the dominant or recessive allele (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig3">3a,b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">15</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Meng, L., Li, H., Zhang, L. &amp; Wang, J. QTL IciMapping: integrated software for genetic linkage map construction and quantitative trait locus mapping in biparental populations. Crop J. 3, 269‚Äì283 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1060">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Arends, D., Prins, P., Jansen, R. C. &amp; Broman, K. W. R/qtl: high-throughput multiple QTL mapping. Bioinformatics 26, 2990‚Äì2992 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1063">41</a></sup>. All six loci were located in the SD regions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">13</a>). Generally, large-effect deleterious mutations are relatively dispersed in the genome, which could be removed by sexual selection.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Tight linkage of two deleterious genes in the repulsion phase."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Tight linkage of two deleterious genes in the repulsion phase.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0699-x/MediaObjects/41588_2020_699_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0699-x/MediaObjects/41588_2020_699_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="590" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Phenotype of normal seedling (<i>WS1</i>) and white seedling (<i>ws1</i>), normal plant architecture (<i>PA1</i>) and more branched architecture (<i>pa1</i>). <b>b</b>, Genetic mapping of <i>PA1</i> and <i>ws1</i> on chromosome 1_1. The top graph shows the likelihood of odd (LOD) value of <i>PA1</i> mapping using R/qtl software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Arends, D., Prins, P., Jansen, R. C. &amp; Broman, K. W. R/qtl: high-throughput multiple QTL mapping. Bioinformatics 26, 2990‚Äì2992 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1109">41</a></sup>, and the bottom graph represents the number of individuals with a homozygous recessive allele. Dots in the graphs present the genetic markers. The <i>ar1</i> locus has been reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhang, C. et al. The genetic basis of inbreeding depression in potato. Nat. Genet. 51, 374‚Äì378 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1116">39</a></sup>. <b>c</b>, Fine mapping of <i>ws1</i> and <i>pa1</i> using indel markers (green bars). Gray segments represent the repeat elements; green and yellow blocks indicate the positions of <i>WS1/ws1</i> and <i>PA1/pa1</i>, respectively. <b>d</b>, Quantitative PCR (qPCR) result of <i>RHC01H1G0699.2</i> in normal (<i>PA1</i>/<i>PA1</i>) and more-branched (<i>pa1</i>/<i>pa1</i>) plants. Error bars represent the standard deviation from four biological replicates, and asterisks indicate significant differences between normal and more-branched plants (<i>t</i>-test, **<i>P</i> value‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ0.01). <b>e</b>, CDS alignment of <i>RHC01H1G0699.2</i>, <i>RHC01H2G0765.2</i> and their homolog in DM (<i>DMG400008712</i>), showing the 57-bp indel between alleles.</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM7">Source data</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>However, two recessive detrimental alleles, <i>ws1</i> and <i>pa1</i>, are tightly linked but in repulsion on the short arm of chromosome 1. Phenotype-based selection will not be sufficient to break the linkage of these two alleles, as demonstrated in practical breeding efforts; therefore, additional genetic analysis of this locus is required. In the mapping of <i>pa1</i> and <i>ws1</i>, using 880 F<sub>2</sub> progeny, the two loci remain linked (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig3">3b</a>). Genotyping of an additional 1,200 F<sub>2</sub> individuals identified two recombinant plants, which delimited <i>ws1</i> and <i>pa1</i> into two adjacent regions. The <i>WS1</i> locus was mapped to a 1.19‚ÄâMb region (chr1_2: 12,061,229‚Äì13,249,054) that contained 76 annotated genes, including six DEL and 30 allelic genes harboring exonic variants. The <i>PA1</i> locus was mapped to a 191-kb region (chr1_1: 11,821,758‚Äì12,012,928) containing 11 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig3">3c</a>). Among them, one gene, <i>RHC01H1G0699.2</i>, encoding the ETHYLENE INSENSITIVE3 (EIN3) protein was identified as a candidate. <i>AtEIN3</i> has been reported to regulate plant growth in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Munne-Bosch, S., Simancas, B. &amp; Muller, M. Ethylene signaling cross-talk with other hormones in Arabidopsis thaliana exposed to contrasting phosphate availability: differential effects in roots, leaves and fruits. J. Plant Physiol. 226, 114‚Äì122 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1233">42</a></sup> by acting as a transcriptional regulator in the ethylene signaling pathway. <i>RHC01H1G0699.2</i> showed decreased expression in more-branched plants (<i>pa1</i>/<i>pa1</i>) than in normal plants (<i>PA1</i>/<i>PA1</i>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig3">3d</a>), and this expression pattern was consistent with observations on the <i>A. thaliana ein3</i> mutant. Between the dominant allele <i>RHC01H1G0699.2</i> and the recessive allele <i>RHC01H2G0765.2</i>, there was a 57-bp insertion that might result in additional translation of 19 amino acids in RHC01H2G0765.2 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#Fig3">3e</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">17</a>). Genome-assisted analyses of these two large-effect deleterious alleles provide tools to break the tight linkage in the repulsive phase for a better inbred line from RH.</p><p>In the current study, we combined multiple sequencing technologies to achieve the de novo assembly and haplotype determination of the heterozygous diploid potato. Compared with short reads or the longer but more error-prone ONT reads, CCS reads generated higher resolution and accuracy in differentiating haplotypes, which is particularly useful in resolving complex genomes. However, for a complex genome like diploid potato, there is still no tool that can build near-complete haplotypes from long-read sequencing or Hi-C sequencing without the assistance of genetic information, which requires further improvement in assembly algorithms.</p><p>The haplotype-resolved genome of the diploid potato provides a holistic view of the genome organization of a clonally propagated, heterozygous plant species. Haplotype-resolved identification of deleterious mutations, especially tightly linked genes in repulsion, provides insights into purging mutation burden by efficient molecular selection and/or genome-editing technologies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kremling, K. A. G. et al. Dysregulation of expression correlates with rare-allele burden and fitness loss in maize. Nature 555, 520‚Äì523 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1281">43</a></sup>. As such, this study could facilitate the exploitation of heterosis, using inbred lines with complementary haplotypes, which is the core of diploid potato breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome, transcriptome and methylome sequencing</h3><p>To construct an Illumina sequencing library, genomic DNA was extracted from RH leaves by using the cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Gawel, N. J. &amp; Jarret, R. L. A modified CTAB DNA extraction procedure for Musa and Ipomoea. Plant Mol. Biol. Report. 9, 262‚Äì266 (1991)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1297">44</a></sup>. The library was sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform, generating 155‚ÄâGb of 250-nucleotide paired-end reads with an insert size of ~400‚Äâbp.</p><p>About 1.2‚Äâng high molecular weight DNA (&gt;50‚Äâkb) was isolated and loaded for 10xG library construction, following the manufacturer‚Äôs recommended protocols (<a href="https://support.10xgenomics.com/de-novo-assembly/library-prep/doc/user-guide-chromium-genome-reagent-kit-v1-chemistry">https://support.10xgenomics.com/de-novo-assembly/library-prep/doc/user-guide-chromium-genome-reagent-kit-v1-chemistry/</a>). The 10xG library was sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform, yielding 122‚ÄâGb of 150-nucleotide paired-end data.</p><p>In construction of the ONT library, an optimized protocol for long plant DNA enrichment was applied<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Schmidt, M. H. et al. De novo assembly of a new Solanum pennellii accession using nanopore sequencing. Plant Cell 29, 2336‚Äì2348 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1314">45</a></sup>. The library was constructed using LSK108 kit (SQK-LSK108, Oxford) and sequenced using 38 R9.4 flow cells on the Nanopore GridION X5 sequencer. The base calling was performed using Albacore in MinKNOW package, and 10.7 million nanopore reads with an N50 length of 25.3‚Äâkb were available for assembly.</p><p>For CCS, genomic DNA was extracted from in vitro seedlings using the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen). The integrity of the DNA was determined with the Agilent 4200 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies). Genomic DNA (15‚ÄâŒºg) was sheared using g-Tubes (Covaris) and concentrated with AMPure PB magnetic beads. Two SMRTbell libraries were constructed using the Pacific Biosciences SMRTbell Template Prep Kit 1.0. The libraries were size selected on a BluePippin system for molecules with a size of 11‚ÄâKb, followed by primer annealing and the binding of SMRTbell templates to polymerases with the DNA/Polymerase Binding Kit. Libraries sequencing was carried out on the Pacific Bioscience Sequel II platform (Annoroad Gene Technology) and 29-Gb CCS reads with an N50 size of 13‚Äâkb were generated using ccs software v.3.0.0 (<a href="https://github.com/pacificbiosciences/unanimity/">https://github.com/pacificbiosciences/unanimity/</a>).</p><p>The Hi-C libraries were constructed at Annoroad Gene Technology using the in situ method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Lajoie, B. R., Dekker, J. &amp; Kaplan, N. The Hitchhiker‚Äôs guide to Hi-C analysis: practical guidelines. Methods 72, 65‚Äì75 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1332">46</a></sup>. DNA from in vitro seedlings of RH was digested with MboI using the standard Hi-C library preparation protocol. The Hi-C libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform, yielding 150‚ÄâGb of data.</p><p>The selfing population (S<sub>1</sub> population, equivalent to an F<sub>2</sub> population) of RH was constructed by forced self-pollination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhang, C. et al. The genetic basis of inbreeding depression in potato. Nat. Genet. 51, 374‚Äì378 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1343">39</a></sup> and 880 F<sub>2</sub> individuals were sequenced at ~1√ó depth using an Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform. On average, ~2‚ÄâGb of data were obtained from each individual.</p><p>Samples from the young leaf, mature leaf, stem, perianth, anther, carpel, stolon, immature small tuber (transection diameter of &lt;1‚Äâcm), immature big tuber (transection diameter of 1‚Äì5‚Äâcm) and root tissue were collected for transcriptome sequencing. All tissues were isolated and sequenced in three biological replicates. Total RNA was extracted from the samples using the TIANGEN Kit with DNase I and processed for the library construction using NEBNext UltraTM RNA Library Prep Kit. Following the removal of low-quality data, ~3‚ÄâGb of 150-nucleotide paired-end data for each sample were used for further RNA-seq analysis.</p><p>In addition to the transcriptome sequencing, samples from three tissues‚Äîmature leaf, immature small tuber (transection diameter &lt;1‚Äâcm) and immature large tuber (transection diameter of 1‚Äì5‚Äâcm)‚Äîwere used for whole-genome bisulfite sequencing with three biological replicates. Genomic DNA was extracted using the CTAB method and fragmented to ~200‚Äì300‚Äâbp by sonication before library construction. The barcoded DNA was treated twice with bisulfite using the EZ DNA Methylation-Gold Kit (Zymo Research) according to the manufacturer‚Äôs instructions. The libraries were sequenced on a HiSeq X Ten platform and 50‚ÄâGb of 150-nucleotide paired-end data were generated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Hierarchical assembly and phasing of diploid potato genome using 10xG and ONT data</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec5">Whole-genome de novo assembly</h4><p>The WGS Illumina reads were assembled using DISCOVAR de novo (<a href="https://software.broadinstitute.org/software/discovar/blog/">https://software.broadinstitute.org/software/discovar/blog/</a>), resulting in 1.3-Gb sequences with a scaffold N50 size of 14.9‚Äâkb. The 10xG reads were assembled using Supernova (<a href="https://github.com/10XGenomics/supernova">https://github.com/10XGenomics/supernova/</a>) with the ‚Äòmegabubbles‚Äô output; 1.58‚ÄâGb of assembled sequence data were generated.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec6">Genetic genotyping and grouping</h4><p>In the pipeline, the 880 F<sub>2</sub> selfing progeny were sequenced at ~1√ó‚Äì2√ó coverage for genetic mapping (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">5</a>). First, for each progeny, the sequenced reads were mapped to the assembled RH scaffolds using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997v2&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1394">47</a></sup>. For each scaffold, the raw number of mapped reads with mapping quality &gt;50 was normalized according to the scaffold length, the total assembled length and the total mapped reads of this progeny. Then the normalized read number for all scaffolds was transformed to genotype scores. The genetic groups were built using the software JoinMap (v4.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Van Ooijen, J. W. Multipoint maximum likelihood mapping in a full-sib family of an outbreeding species. Genet. Res. 93, 343‚Äì349 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1398">48</a></sup>. A total of 1,408‚ÄâMb sequences were genotyped and grouped into 12 linkage groups, corresponding to the 12 chromosomes of monoploid potato.</p><p>For each linkage group, we applied the R function <i>hclust</i> (method‚Äâ=‚Äâ‚Äòward.D2‚Äô, <i>k</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ2) to separate each group into two clusters, corresponding to the two haplotypes of the diploid potato. To assign the residual 310‚ÄâMb of scaffolds, which displayed obscure read distribution and failed in genotype calling, we calculated the correlation between grouped scaffolds (target) and residual scaffolds (query) on the number of mapped reads using the <i>cor</i> function in R (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">5</a>). If the query scaffold and the target scaffold shared a similar pattern (correlation‚Äâ&gt;‚Äâ0.7) on read distribution in the population, they were deemed to belong to same linkage group. For each query scaffold, we determined its group using two criteria: (1) the top two correlation values with target scaffolds should be larger than 0.7 and (2) the top two target scaffolds showing the highest correlation values should be located on the same group. After this process, 117.8‚ÄâMb of residual scaffolds were assigned to 24 linkage groups. In total, 1.52‚ÄâGb of 1.7‚ÄâGb sequences were grouped into 24 clusters, accounting for 90% of the assembled genome.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec7">Simplified reassembly within each group</h4><p>One effective way to simplify the assembly for a complex genome is to dilute the genome into multiple parts and separately assemble each part. In this project, we leveraged a similar simplification by separately reassembling the 24 clusters. First, the ONT long reads were mapped to the scaffolds using minimap2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, H. &amp; Birol, I. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 34, 3094‚Äì3100 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1425">49</a></sup>). Second, the reads belonging to each genetic group were retrieved and assembled into contigs using SMARTdenovo (<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo">https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo/</a>). Only reads with properly paired mapping and less than two mismatched bases reads were collected for the reassembly. Third, the contigs were polished iteratively using Racon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Vaser, R., Soviƒá, I., Nagarajan, N. &amp; ≈†ikiƒá, M. Fast and accurate de novo genome assembly from long uncorrected reads. Genome Res. 27, 737‚Äì746 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1436">22</a></sup> and Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Wang, J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1440">23</a></sup>. Last, the 10xG reads were aligned to the contigs using Long Ranger (<a href="https://support.10xgenomics.com/genome-exome/software/downloads/latest">https://support.10xgenomics.com/genome-exome/software/downloads/latest</a>) to generate scaffolds using the ARCS‚Äâ+‚ÄâLINKS pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Yeo, S. et al. ARCS: scaffolding genome drafts with linked reads. Bioinformatics 34, 725‚Äì731 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1452">50</a></sup>, which increased the assembly continuity from contig N50 length of 636‚Äâkb to 921‚Äâkb. The hybrid assembly yielded the genome draft RHgv1.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genome assembly and phasing using PacBio CCS reads</h3><p>A total of 29‚ÄâGb CCS reads were assembled using Canu (v1.91)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722‚Äì736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1465">25</a></sup> with the parameter --pacbio-hifi. Canu generated two assemblies composed of contigs and unitigs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">4</a>), and the unitig assembly consisted of the contigs that split at any alternative paths in the assembly graph. The contig assembly had longer continuity but more chimeric fragments as revealed in the genetic mapping analysis. To avoid the mis-joining of two haplotypes, the unitig assembly rather than the contig assembly was chosen for the subsequent analysis. The unitigs were then polished iteratively using two rounds of Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Wang, J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1472">23</a></sup> with ~150‚ÄâGb of WGS Illumina data, generating the genome draft RHgv2.</p><p>Similarly, the sequenced reads of RH selfing progeny were mapped to unitigs of RHgv2 to perform genetic grouping. Because the unitigs were relatively long (N50‚Äâ=‚Äâ2‚ÄâMb), windows with a size of 200‚Äâkb rather than the whole unitig were used. If the adjacent windows of one unitig showed contrary read distribution, the unitig was defined as chimeric and broken between windows; 40 chimeric unitigs with a total length of 95‚ÄâMb were broken. In total, 1.31‚ÄâGb of 1.53‚ÄâGb sequences were assigned to 24 linkage groups.</p><p>After merging, 141‚ÄâMb sequences and 5,252 annotated genes of RHgv1 were added to the RHgv2, yielding a 1.67‚ÄâGb genome draft with 1.54‚ÄâGb sequences assigned to 24 groups, termed RHgv3 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM1">6</a>). The sequences from the RHgv1 and RHgv2 assemblies were named as ontctg* and unitig* in the AGP file, respectively (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM5">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Construction of pseudochromosomes</h3><p>As no approach generated satisfactory results on the RH genome, we introduced the group information derived from the genetic mapping to assist the Hi-C application on chromosome-level assembly. The process was performed on RHgv3 including three steps as follows:</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
<li>
<span class="u-custom-list-number">1.</span>

<p>Align. The 24 previously determined groups were divided into two haplotypes to generate two pseudohaploid genome drafts. The 132‚ÄâMb sequences that could not be assigned to any group were added to two pseudohaploid genomes. Total Hi-C reads were aligned to each pseudohaploid genome using HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1506">51</a></sup> to calculate the contact frequency. This step yielded two bam files for the two pseudohaploid genomes.</p>

</li>
<li>
<span class="u-custom-list-number">2.</span>

<p>Rescue. Using the bam file as input, the <i>rescue</i> function in ALLHiC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhang, X., Zhang, S., Zhao, Q., Ming, R. &amp; Tang, H. Assembly of allele-aware, chromosomal-scale autopolyploid genomes based on Hi-C data. Nat. Plants 5, 833‚Äì845 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1524">20</a></sup> was applied to assign unplaced sequences to known groups. Because the 132‚ÄâMb unplaced sequences were added to two pseudohaploid genomes and processed twice, the rescued results were redundant. For every unplaced sequence, we considered its best Hi-C signal density to decide the group to which it belonged. After this step, the sequence content of 24 groups was updated with an extra 75.6‚ÄâMb sequences assigned to proper groups.</p>

</li>
<li>
<span class="u-custom-list-number">3.</span>

<p>Optimize and build. For each pseudohaploid genome, using the bam file and the updated group file as input, the <i>optimize</i> function in ALLHiC decided the order and orientation of scaffolds for each group; thus, the <i>build</i> function generated fasta sequences on that basis. By performing this step, we identified the pseudochromosomes for 24 groups. The order and orientation of scaffolds on chromosomes are provided in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM5">3</a>.</p>

</li>
</ol><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome assembly assessment</h3><p>The BAC clones and BEs of RH were downloaded from <a href="http://solanaceae.plantbiology.msu.edu/pgsc_download.shtml">http://solanaceae.plantbiology.msu.edu/pgsc_download.shtml</a> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189‚Äì195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1567">6</a></sup>) to assess the assembly.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec11">Assess scaffold assembly</h4><p>Using Sanger technology, the 54,902 paired BEs were sequenced with the average length of 714 nucleotides<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189‚Äì195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1578">6</a></sup>. The BEs were aligned to the assembled scaffolds using BLASR (v1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Chaisson, M. J. &amp; Tesler, G. Mapping single molecule sequencing reads using basic local alignment with successive refinement (BLASR): application and theory. BMC Bioinformatics 13, 238 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1582">52</a></sup>, and those aligned with &gt;98% query coverage and &gt;98% identity were considered as the successful alignments. A reasonable distance between end sequences (set to ~30‚Äì300‚Äâkb) and correct orientation (¬± for each of them), when mapped on to the genome, were used as criteria for assessing the correctness of the assemblies.</p><p>The 184 BACs that assembled with ordered contigs and a total length of 21,734,426‚Äâbp were aligned to RH scaffolds using MUMmer (v3.23)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1589">29</a></sup>. The alignment was filtered using the cutoff criteria: identity &gt;98% and alignment length &gt;2‚Äâkb. Some BACs completely aligned with single scaffolds, while others were fragmented or repeatedly mapped to multiple scaffolds. To evaluate the correctness of scaffolds or contigs, only the BACs that mapped to single scaffolds of genome assembly were used in the statistics. The mapping structure between BACs and scaffolds was manually checked to determine if the alignments were complete and collinear (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM3">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#MOESM4">2</a>). SNPs and indels were identified from the alignment between BACs and scaffolds using the <i>show-snps</i> function in MUMmer.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec12">Assess phasing quality</h4><p>The BE sequences and 1,639 RH BACs with a total length of 205‚ÄâMb and an average size of 125‚Äâkb were aligned to the pseudochromosomes of RHgv3 using BLASR (v5.1) to assess the haplotypes. Because most of the BACs contained only unordered contigs, we considered the alignment length and identity when selecting the best hits for BACs. Only alignments with a mapQV of &gt;50 and identity of &gt;95% were retained for downstream analyses.</p><p>A total of 55.4‚ÄâMb nonredundant BAC MTPs with an N50 length of 336‚Äâkb were extracted from the assembly of the previously published RH chromosome 5 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="de Boer, J. M. et al. Homologues of potato chromosome 5 show variable collinearity in the euchromatin, but dramatic absence of sequence similarity in the pericentromeric heterochromatin. BMC Genomics 16, 374 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1613">21</a></sup>). MTPs were aligned to the RH chromosomes using MUMmer (v3.23)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1617">29</a></sup> and filtered using the criteria of identity &gt;95% and alignment length &gt;2‚Äâkb. For each MTP, only the best hit was considered to compare the phases.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome annotation</h3><p>Repeat-sequence masking was performed using RepeatMasker (v4.0.6) with default parameters. The reference repeat libraries included plant short fragment repeats and DM annotated repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189‚Äì195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1630">6</a></sup>. The RNA-seq data were aligned to the reference genome with HISAT2 (v2.0.4) and assembled using StringTie (v1.2.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650‚Äì1667 (2016)." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1634">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357‚Äì360 (2015)." href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1634_1">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290‚Äì295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1637">55</a></sup>. Trinity (v2.4.0) was used to assemble transcripts with (--genome_guided_max_intron 15,000 --genome_guided_bam --min_kmer_cov 2 --trimmomatic --normalize_reads) and without (--min_kmer_cov 2 --trimmomatic --normalize_reads --no_bowtie) reference guidance. To perform ab initio gene prediction, PASA (v2.2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Haas, B. J. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654‚Äì5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1641">56</a></sup> was used to build the coding region model. This PASA step utilized assembled transcripts from Trinity as the library and trained the model. The ab initio predictions included SNAP (--categorize 100, --export 1,000 and --plus)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1645">57</a></sup>, AUGUSTUS (v2.7)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W309‚ÄìW312 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1649">58</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM (v3.0.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878‚Äì2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0699-x#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d4680e1654">59</a></sup>.
